<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Violence</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Use of physical force or power with the intent to inflict harm</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Violence_(disambiguation)" title="Violence (disambiguation)">Violence (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Violent" redirects here. For the British destroyer, see <a href="/wiki/HMS_Violent_(D57)" title="HMS Violent (D57)">HMS <i>Violent</i> (D57)</a>. For the film, see <a href="/wiki/Violent_(film)" title="Violent (film)"><i>Violent</i> (film)</a>. For the song by Stellar, see <a href="/wiki/Violent_(song)" title="Violent (song)">Violent (song)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nilgais_fighting,_Lakeshwari,_Gwalior_district,_India.jpg"><img alt="See caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2393" data-file-width="3745" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nilgais_fighting%2C_Lakeshwari%2C_Gwalior_district%2C_India.jpg/330px-Nilgais_fighting%2C_Lakeshwari%2C_Gwalior_district%2C_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nilgais_fighting%2C_Lakeshwari%2C_Gwalior_district%2C_India.jpg/495px-Nilgais_fighting%2C_Lakeshwari%2C_Gwalior_district%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Nilgais_fighting%2C_Lakeshwari%2C_Gwalior_district%2C_India.jpg/660px-Nilgais_fighting%2C_Lakeshwari%2C_Gwalior_district%2C_India.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nilgais_fighting,_Lakeshwari,_Gwalior_district,_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two male <a href="/wiki/Nilgai" title="Nilgai">nilgais</a> fighting each other using their horns</div></div></div>
<p><b>Violence</b> is the use of physical force so as to injure, abuse, damage, or destroy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Other definitions are also used, such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>'s definition of violence as "the intentional use of physical force or <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">power</a>, threatened<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> or actual, against oneself, another person, or against a group or community, which either results in or has a high likelihood of resulting in injury, death, psychological harm, maldevelopment, or deprivation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Internationally, violence resulted in deaths of an estimated 1.28 million people in 2013 up from 1.13 million in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-4">[4]</a></sup> However, global population grew by roughly 1.9 billion during those years, showing a dramatic reduction in violence per capita. Of the deaths in 2013, roughly 842,000 were attributed to self-harm (<a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>), 405,000 to interpersonal violence, and 31,000 to collective violence (<a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>) and legal intervention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-4">[4]</a></sup> For each single death due to violence, there are dozens of hospitalizations, hundreds of emergency department visits, and thousands of doctors' appointments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Furthermore, violence often has lifelong consequences for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_health" title="Physical health">physical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> and social functioning and can slow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic" title="Economic">economic</a> and social development.
</p><p>In 2013, of the estimated 405,000 deaths due to interpersonal violence globally, assault by <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearm</a> was the cause in 180,000 deaths, assault by sharp object was the cause in 114,000 deaths, and the remaining 110,000 deaths from other causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Violence in many forms can be preventable. There is a strong relationship between levels of violence and modifiable factors in a country such as an <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_poverty" title="Concentrated poverty">concentrated (regional) poverty</a>, income and <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">gender inequality</a>, the harmful use of alcohol, and the absence of safe, stable, and nurturing relationships between children and parents. Strategies addressing the underlying causes of violence can be relatively effective in preventing violence, although mental and physical health and individual responses, personalities, etc. have always been decisive factors in the formation of these behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Typology_of_violence.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="1099" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Typology_of_violence.jpg/220px-Typology_of_violence.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Typology_of_violence.jpg/330px-Typology_of_violence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Typology_of_violence.jpg/440px-Typology_of_violence.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Typology_of_violence.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typology of violence<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The World Health Organization divides violence into three broad categories:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>self-directed violence</li>
<li>interpersonal violence</li>
<li>collective violence</li></ul>
<p>This initial categorization differentiates between violence that a person inflicts upon themself, violence inflicted by another individual or by a small group of individuals, and violence inflicted by larger groups such as states, organized political groups, militia groups and terrorist organizations.
</p><p>Alternatively, violence can primarily be classified as either instrumental or reactive / hostile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-directed">Self-directed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Self-directed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Self-directed violence is subdivided into suicidal behaviour and <a href="/wiki/Self-harm" title="Self-harm">self-abuse</a>. The former includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suicidal_thoughts" title="Suicidal thoughts">suicidal thoughts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attempted_suicide" title="Attempted suicide">attempted suicides</a>—also called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Para_suicide" title="Para suicide">para suicide</a></i> or <i>deliberate self-injury</i> in some countries—and suicide itself. Self-abuse, in contrast, includes acts such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-mutilation" title="Self-mutilation">self-mutilation</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collective">Collective</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Collective">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1065" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg/220px-The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg/330px-The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg/440px-The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Bochnia_massacre_German-occupied_Poland_1939.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Massacre" title="Massacre">Massacre</a> of <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Polish</a> civilians during <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Poland_(1939%E2%80%931945)" title="Occupation of Poland (1939–1945)">Nazi Germany's occupation of Poland</a>, December 1939</div></div></div>
<p>Unlike the other two broad categories, the subcategories of collective violence suggest possible motives for violence committed by larger groups of individuals or by states. Collective violence that is committed to advance a particular social agenda includes, for example, crimes of hate committed by organized groups, terrorist acts and mob violence. <a href="/wiki/Political_violence" title="Political violence">Political violence</a> includes war and related violent conflicts, state violence and similar acts carried out by armed groups. There may be multiple <a href="/wiki/Determinants_of_violence_against_civilians" title="Determinants of violence against civilians">determinants of violence against civilians</a> in such situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balcells_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balcells-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economic_violence" title="Economic violence">Economic violence</a> includes attacks motivated by economic gain—such as attacks carried out with the purpose of disrupting economic activity, denying access to essential services, or creating economic division and fragmentation. Clearly, acts committed by domestic and subnational groups can have multiple motives.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Section on economic violence has no citation (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Slow_violence" title="Slow violence">Slow violence</a> is a long-duration form of violence which is often invisible (at least to those not impacted by it), such as environmental degradation, pollution and climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UStankParis-edit1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2853" data-file-width="2265" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/UStankParis-edit1.jpg/220px-UStankParis-edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/UStankParis-edit1.jpg/330px-UStankParis-edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/UStankParis-edit1.jpg/440px-UStankParis-edit1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UStankParis-edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A United States <a href="/wiki/M8_Greyhound" title="M8 Greyhound">M8 Greyhound</a> armoured car in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> during World War II</div></div></div>
<p>War is a state of prolonged violent large-scale conflict involving two or more groups of people, usually under the auspices of government. It is the most extreme form of collective violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
War is fought as a means of resolving territorial and other conflicts, as <a href="/wiki/War_of_aggression" title="War of aggression">war of aggression</a> to conquer territory or loot resources, in <a href="/wiki/Defensive_war" title="Defensive war">national self-defence</a> or liberation, or to suppress attempts of part of the nation to <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secede</a> from it. There are also ideological, <a href="/wiki/Religious_war" title="Religious war">religious</a> and <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolutionary wars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> the lethality of modern warfare has grown. <a href="/wiki/World_War_I_casualties" title="World War I casualties">World War I casualties</a> were over 40 million and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties" title="World War II casualties">World War II casualties</a> were over 70 million.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpersonal">Interpersonal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Interpersonal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_(Courtauld_Institute).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9694" data-file-width="7045" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_%28Courtauld_Institute%29.jpg/220px-Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_%28Courtauld_Institute%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_%28Courtauld_Institute%29.jpg/330px-Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_%28Courtauld_Institute%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_%28Courtauld_Institute%29.jpg/440px-Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_%28Courtauld_Institute%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peter_Paul_Rubens_-_Cain_slaying_Abel_(Courtauld_Institute).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cain killing Abel</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="905" data-file-width="1128" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png/220px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png/330px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png/440px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_093.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Saul attacks David</i> (who had been playing music to help Saul feel better), 1860 woodcut by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julius_Schnorr_von_Karolsfeld" title="Julius Schnorr von Karolsfeld">Julius Schnorr von Karolsfeld</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Interpersonal violence</b> is divided into two subcategories: Family and <a href="/wiki/Intimate_partner_violence" title="Intimate partner violence">intimate partner violence</a>—that is, violence largely between family members and intimate partners, usually, though not exclusively, taking place in the home. Community violence—violence between individuals who are unrelated, and who may or may not know each other, generally taking place outside the home. The former group includes forms of violence such as <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">child abuse</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment_of_children" title="Corporal punishment of children">child corporal punishment</a>, intimate partner violence and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elderly_abuse" title="Elderly abuse">abuse of the elderly</a>. The latter includes youth violence, random acts of violence, <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sexual_assault" title="Sexual assault">sexual assault</a> by strangers, and violence in institutional settings such as schools, workplaces, prisons and nursing homes. When interpersonal violence occurs in families, its psychological consequences can affect parents, children, and their relationship in the short- and long-terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_maltreatment">Child maltreatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Child maltreatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">Child abuse</a></div>
<p>Child maltreatment is the abuse and neglect that occurs to children under 18 years of age. It includes all types of physical and/or emotional ill-treatment, <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neglect" title="Neglect">neglect</a>, <a href="/wiki/Negligence" title="Negligence">negligence</a> and commercial or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_exploitation" title="Child exploitation">child exploitation</a>, which results in actual or potential harm to the child's health, survival, development or dignity in the context of a relationship of responsibility, trust, or power. Exposure to intimate partner violence is also sometimes included as a form of child maltreatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Child maltreatment is a global problem with serious lifelong consequences, which is, however, complex and difficult to study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are no reliable global estimates for the prevalence of child maltreatment. Data for many countries, especially low- and middle-income countries, are lacking. Current estimates vary widely depending on the country and the method of research used. Approximately 20% of women and 5–10% of men report being sexually abused as children, while 25–50% of all children report being physically abused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Consequences of child maltreatment include impaired lifelong physical and mental health, and social and occupational functioning (e.g. school, job, and relationship difficulties). These can ultimately slow a country's economic and social development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Preventing child maltreatment before it starts is possible and requires a multisectoral approach. Effective prevention programmes support parents and teach positive parenting skills. Ongoing care of children and families can reduce the risk of maltreatment reoccurring and can minimize its consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Youth">Youth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Youth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG/220px-Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG/330px-Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG/440px-Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kids_off_the_Block_Rocks_in_grass_IMG_4815.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kids_off_the_Block" title="Kids off the Block">Kids off the Block</a> memorial featuring hundreds of simple stone blocks, one for each child killed by violence in <a href="/wiki/Roseland,_Chicago" title="Roseland, Chicago">Roseland, Chicago</a></div></div></div>
<p>Following the World Health Organization, youth are defined as people between the ages of 10 and 29 years. Youth violence refers to violence occurring between youths, and includes acts that range from <a href="/wiki/Bullying" title="Bullying">bullying</a> and physical fighting, through more severe sexual and physical assault to homicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mercy,_J.A._2002_pp_23_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mercy,_J.A._2002_pp_23-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Worldwide some 250,000 homicides occur among youth 10–29 years of age each year, which is 41% of the total number of homicides globally each year ("Global Burden of Disease", World Health Organization, 2008). For each young person killed, 20–40 more sustain injuries requiring hospital treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mercy,_J.A._2002_pp_23_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mercy,_J.A._2002_pp_23-20">[20]</a></sup> Youth violence has a serious, often lifelong, impact on a person's psychological and social functioning. Youth violence greatly increases the costs of health, welfare and criminal justice services; reduces productivity; decreases the value of property; and generally undermines the fabric of society.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (July 2020)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Prevention programmes shown to be effective or to have promise in reducing youth violence include life skills and social development programmes designed to help children and adolescents manage anger, resolve conflict, and develop the necessary social skills to solve problems; schools-based anti-bullying prevention programmes; and programmes to reduce access to alcohol, illegal drugs and guns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Also, given significant neighbourhood effects on youth violence, interventions involving relocating families to less poor environments have shown promising results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leventhal_and_Brooks-Gunn_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leventhal_and_Brooks-Gunn-23">[23]</a></sup> Similarly, urban renewal projects such as <a href="/wiki/Business_improvement_district" title="Business improvement district">business improvement districts</a> have shown a reduction in youth violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rand_report_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rand_report-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different types of youth on youth violence include witnessing or being involved in physical, emotional and sexual abuse (e.g. physical attacks, bullying, rape), and violent acts like gang shootings and robberies. According to researchers in 2018, "More than half of children and adolescents living in cities have experienced some form of community violence." The violence "can also all take place under one roof, or in a given community or neighborhood and can happen at the same time or at different stages of life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Conversation_US_Darby_Saxbe_2018_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Conversation_US_Darby_Saxbe_2018-25">[25]</a></sup> Youth violence has immediate and long term adverse impact whether the individual was the recipient of the violence or a witness to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Youth violence impacts individuals, their families, and society. Victims can have lifelong injuries which means ongoing doctor and hospital visits, the cost of which quickly add up. Since the victims of youth-on-youth violence may not be able to attend school or work because of their physical and/or mental injuries, it is often up to their family members to take care of them, including paying their daily living expenses and medical bills. Their caretakers may have to give up their jobs or work reduced hours to provide help to the victim of violence. This causes a further burden on society because the victim and maybe even their caretakers have to obtain government assistance to help pay their bills. Recent research has found that psychological trauma during childhood can change a child's brain. "Trauma is known to physically affect the brain and the body which causes anxiety, rage, and the ability to concentrate. They can also have problems remembering, trusting, and forming relationships."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Since the brain becomes used to violence it may stay continually in an alert state (similar to being stuck in the fight or flight mode). "Researchers claim that the youth who are exposed to violence may have emotional, social, and cognitive problems. They may have trouble controlling emotions, paying attention in school, withdraw from friends, or show signs of post-traumatic stress disorder".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Conversation_US_Darby_Saxbe_2018_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Conversation_US_Darby_Saxbe_2018-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is important for youth exposed to violence to understand how their bodies may react so they can take positive steps to counteract any possible short- and long-term negative effects (e.g., poor concentration, feelings of depression, heightened levels of anxiety). By taking immediate steps to mitigate the effects of the trauma they've experienced, negative repercussions can be reduced or eliminated. As an initial step, the youths need to understand why they may be feeling a certain way and to understand how the violence they have experienced may be causing negative feelings and making them behave differently. Pursuing a greater awareness of their feelings, perceptions, and negative emotions is the first step that should be taken as part of recovering from the trauma they have experienced. "Neuroscience research shows that the only way we can change the way we feel is by becoming aware of our inner experience and learning to befriend what is going on inside ourselves".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Body_Keeps_The_Score_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Body_Keeps_The_Score-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the ways to combat the adverse effects of exposure to youth violence would be to try various mindfulness and movement activities, deep breathing exercises and other actions that enable youths to release their pent up emotions. Using these techniques will teach body awareness, reduce anxiety and nervousness, and reduce feelings of anger and annoyance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Over time these types of activities will help these younger victims of violence to have greater control over their feelings and behaviors and avoid unhealthy ways of coping. Another way to help trauma victims of youth violence is through the arts. This can be accomplished by giving them the opportunity to engage in drawing, painting, music, and singing which will give them an outlet to express themselves and their emotions in a positive way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Youth who have experienced violence benefit from having a close relationship with one or more people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Body_Keeps_The_Score_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Body_Keeps_The_Score-28">[28]</a></sup> This is important because the trauma victims need to have people who are safe and trustworthy that they can relate and talk to about their horrible experiences. Some youth do not have adult figures at home or someone they can count on for guidance and comfort. Schools in bad neighborhoods where youth violence is prevalent should assign counselors to each student so that they receive regular guidance. In addition to counseling/therapy sessions and programs, it has been recommended that schools offer mentoring programs where students can interact with adults who can be a positive influence on them. Another way is to create more neighborhood programs to ensure that each child has a positive and stable place to go when school in not in session. Many children have benefited from formal organizations now which aim to help mentor and provide a safe environment for the youth especially those living in neighborhoods with higher rates of violence. This includes organizations such as Becoming a Man, CeaseFire Illinois, Chicago Area Project, Little Black Pearl, and Rainbow House".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> These programs are designed to help give the youth a safe place to go, stop the violence from occurring, offering counseling and mentoring to help stop the cycle of violence. If the youth do not have a safe place to go after school hours they will likely get into trouble, receive poor grades, drop out of school and use drugs and alcohol. The gangs look for youth who do not have positive influences in their life and need protection. This is why these programs are so important for the youth to have a safe environment rather than resorting to the streets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intimate_partner">Intimate partner</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Intimate partner">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking,_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking%2C_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg/220px-JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking%2C_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking%2C_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg/330px-JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking%2C_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking%2C_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg/440px-JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking%2C_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JBM-HH_roundtable_addresses_digital_stalking,_ties_to_intimate_partner_violence_150113-A-DZ999-852.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joint_Base_Myer-Henderson_Hall" title="Joint Base Myer-Henderson Hall">Joint Base Myer-Henderson Hall</a> (JBM-HH) roundtable addressing digital stalking, ties to intimate partner violence</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">Domestic violence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intimate_partner_violence" title="Intimate partner violence">Intimate partner violence</a></div>
<p>Intimate partner violence refers to behaviour in an intimate relationship that causes physical, sexual or psychological harm, including physical aggression, sexual coercion, psychological abuse and controlling behaviours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Population-level surveys based on reports from victims provide the most accurate estimates of the prevalence of intimate partner violence and sexual violence in non-conflict settings. A study conducted by WHO in 10 mainly developing countries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> found that, among women aged 15 to 49 years, between 15% (Japan) and 70% (Ethiopia and Peru) of women reported physical and/or sexual violence by an intimate partner. A growing body of research on men and intimate partner violence focuses on men as both perpetrators and victims of violence, as well as on how to involve men and boys in anti-violence work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Intimate partner and sexual violence have serious short- and long-term physical, mental, sexual and reproductive health problems for victims and for their children, and lead to high social and economic costs. These include both fatal and non-fatal injuries, depression and post-traumatic stress disorder, <a href="/wiki/Unintended_pregnancy" title="Unintended pregnancy">unintended pregnancies</a>, sexually transmitted infections, including HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Factors associated with the perpetration and experiencing of intimate partner violence are low levels of education, history of violence as a perpetrator, a victim or a witness of parental violence, harmful use of alcohol, attitudes that are accepting of violence as well as marital discord and dissatisfaction. Factors associated only with perpetration of intimate partner violence are having multiple partners, and <a href="/wiki/Antisocial_personality_disorder" title="Antisocial personality disorder">antisocial personality disorder</a>.
</p><p>A recent theory named "The Criminal Spin" suggests a mutual flywheel effect between partners that is manifested by an escalation in the violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> A violent spin may occur in any other forms of violence, but in Intimate partner violence the added value is the mutual spin, based on the unique situation and characteristics of intimate relationship.
</p><p>The primary prevention strategy with the best evidence for effectiveness for intimate partner violence is school-based programming for adolescents to prevent violence within dating relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Evidence is emerging for the effectiveness of several other primary prevention strategies—those that: combine microfinance with gender equality training;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> promote communication and relationship skills within communities; reduce access to, and the harmful use of alcohol; and change cultural gender norms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-who-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual">Sexual</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sexual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">Sexual violence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DRC_raped_women.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/DRC_raped_women.jpg/220px-DRC_raped_women.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/DRC_raped_women.jpg/330px-DRC_raped_women.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/DRC_raped_women.jpg/440px-DRC_raped_women.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DRC_raped_women.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meeting of victims of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence_in_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Sexual violence in the Democratic Republic of the Congo">sexual violence in the Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Sexual violence is any sexual act, attempt to obtain a sexual act, unwanted sexual comments or advances, or acts to traffic, or otherwise directed against a person's sexuality using coercion, by any person regardless of their relationship to the victim, in any setting. It includes rape, defined as the physically forced or otherwise coerced penetration of the vulva or anus with a penis, other body part or object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Population-level surveys based on reports from victims estimate that between 0.3 and 11.5% of women reported experiencing sexual violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Sexual violence has serious short- and long-term consequences on physical, mental, sexual and reproductive health for victims and for their children as described in the section on intimate partner violence. If perpetrated during childhood, sexual violence can lead to increased smoking,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> drug and alcohol misuse, and risky sexual behaviors in later life. It is also associated with perpetration of violence and being a victim of violence.
</p><p>Many of the risk factors for sexual violence are the same as for <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>. Risk factors specific to sexual violence perpetration include beliefs in family honor and sexual purity, ideologies of male sexual entitlement and weak legal sanctions for sexual violence.
</p><p>Few interventions to prevent sexual violence have been demonstrated to be effective. School-based programmes to prevent child sexual abuse by teaching children to recognize and avoid potentially sexually abusive situations are run in many parts of the world and appear promising, but require further research. To achieve lasting change, it is important to enact legislation and develop policies that protect women; address discrimination against women and promote gender equality; and help to move the culture away from violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-who-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elder_maltreatment">Elder maltreatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Elder maltreatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elder_abuse" title="Elder abuse">Elder abuse</a></div>
<p>Elder maltreatment is a single or repeated act, or lack of appropriate action, occurring within any relationship where there is an expectation of trust which causes harm or distress to an older person. This constitutes a violation of human rights and includes <a href="/wiki/Physical_abuse" title="Physical abuse">physical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abuse" title="Sexual abuse">sexual</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychological_abuse" title="Psychological abuse">psychological</a>, emotional; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_abuse" title="Financial abuse">financial</a> and material abuse; abandonment; <a href="/wiki/Neglect" title="Neglect">neglect</a>; and serious loss of <a href="/wiki/Dignity" title="Dignity">dignity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Respect" title="Respect">respect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>While there is little information regarding the extent of maltreatment in elderly populations, especially in developing countries, it is estimated that 4–6% of elderly people in high-income countries have experienced some form of maltreatment at home<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> However, older people are often afraid to report cases of maltreatment to family, friends, or to the authorities. Data on the extent of the problem in institutions such as hospitals, nursing homes and other long-term care facilities are scarce. Elder maltreatment can lead to serious physical injuries and long-term psychological consequences. Elder maltreatment is predicted to increase as many countries are experiencing rapidly ageing populations.
</p><p>Many strategies have been implemented to prevent elder maltreatment and to take action against it and mitigate its consequences including public and professional awareness campaigns, screening (of potential victims and abusers), caregiver support interventions (e.g. stress management, respite care), adult protective services and self-help groups. Their effectiveness has, however, not so far been well-established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Targeted">Targeted</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Targeted">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several rare but painful episodes of <a href="/wiki/Assassination" title="Assassination">assassination</a>, attempted assassination and <a href="/wiki/School_shooting" title="School shooting">school shootings</a> at elementary, middle, high schools, as well as colleges and universities in the United States, led to a considerable body of research on ascertainable behaviors of persons who have planned or carried out such attacks. These studies (1995–2002) investigated what the authors called "targeted violence," described the "path to violence" of those who planned or carried out attacks and laid out suggestions for law enforcement and educators. A major point from these research studies is that targeted violence does not just "come out of the blue".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Everyday">Everyday</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Everyday">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As an anthropological concept, "everyday violence" may refer to the incorporation of different forms of violence (mainly political violence) into daily practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latin_America_and_the_Caribbean" title="Latin America and the Caribbean">Latin America and the Caribbean</a>, the region with the highest murder rate in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> experienced more than 2.5 million murders between 2000 and 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_perspectives">Philosophical perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Philosophical perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some philosophers have argued that any interpretation of reality is intrinsically violent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knowledge_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-knowledge-59">[a]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slavoj_%C5%BDi%C5%BEek" title="Slavoj Žižek">Slavoj Žižek</a> in his book <i>Violence</i> stated that "something violent is the very symbolization of a thing." An <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a> perspective considers the harm inflicted by the very interpretation of the world as a form of violence that is distinct from physical violence in that it is possible to avoid physical violence whereas some ontological violence is intrinsic to all knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Foucault</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Arendt</a> considered the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">power</a> and violence but concluded that while related they are distinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foucault_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-foucault-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 46">: 46 </span></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Feminist_philosophy" title="Feminist philosophy">feminist philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epistemic_injustice" title="Epistemic injustice">epistemic violence</a> is the act of causing harm by an inability to understand the conversation of others due to ignorance. Some philosophers think this will harm marginalized groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epistemic_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-epistemic-62">[c]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_and_models_of_understanding">Factors and models of understanding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Factors and models of understanding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Violence cannot be attributed to solely <a href="/wiki/Protective_factor" title="Protective factor">protective factors</a> or <a href="/wiki/Risk_factor" title="Risk factor">risk factors</a>. Both of these factor groups are equally important in the prevention, intervention, and treatment of violence as a whole. The <a class="external text" href="https://www.cdc.gov/violenceprevention/youthviolence/riskprotectivefactors.html" rel="nofollow">CDC</a> outlines several risk and protective factors for youth violence at the individual, family, social and community levels.
</p><p>Individual risk factors include poor behavioral control, high emotional stress, low IQ, and antisocial beliefs or attitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[60]</a></sup> Family risk factors include authoritarian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childrearing" title="Childrearing">childrearing</a> attitudes, inconsistent disciplinary practices, low emotional attachment to parents or caregivers, and low parental income and involvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[60]</a></sup> Social risk factors include social rejection, poor academic performance and commitment to school, and gang involvement or association with delinquent peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[60]</a></sup> Community risk factors include poverty, low community participation, and diminished economic opportunities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, individual protective factors include an intolerance towards deviance, higher IQ and GPA, elevated popularity and social skills, as well as religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[60]</a></sup> Family protective factors include a connectedness and ability to discuss issues with family members or adults, parent/family use of constructive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coping_strategies" title="Coping strategies">coping strategies</a>, and consistent parental presence during at least one of the following: when awakening, when arriving home from school, at dinner time, or when going to bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[60]</a></sup> Social protective factors include quality school relationships, close relationships with non-deviant peers, involvement in <a href="/wiki/Prosocial_behavior" title="Prosocial behavior">prosocial</a> activities, and exposure to school climates that are: well supervised, use clear behavior rules and disciplinary approaches, and engage parents with teachers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>With many conceptual factors that occur at varying levels in the lives of those impacted, the exact causes of violence are complex. To represent this complexity, the ecological, or <a href="/wiki/Social_ecological_model" title="Social ecological model">social ecological model</a> is often used. The following four-level version of the ecological model is often used in the study of violence:
</p><p>The first level identifies biological and personal factors that influence how individuals behave and increase their likelihood of becoming a victim or perpetrator of violence: demographic characteristics (age, education, income), <a href="/wiki/Genetics_of_aggression" title="Genetics of aggression">genetics</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brain_lesion_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brain lesion theory (page does not exist)">brain lesions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personality_disorders" title="Personality disorders">personality disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substance_abuse" title="Substance abuse">substance abuse</a>, and a history of experiencing, witnessing, or engaging in violent behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCrory_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCrory-65">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The second level focuses on close relationships, such as those with family and friends. In youth violence, for example, having friends who engage in or encourage violence can increase a young person's risk of being a victim or perpetrator of violence. For intimate partner violence, a consistent marker at this level of the model is marital conflict or discord in the relationship. In <a href="/wiki/Elder_abuse" title="Elder abuse">elder abuse</a>, important factors are stress due to the nature of the past relationship between the abused person and the care giver.
</p><p>The third level explores the community context—i.e., schools, workplaces, and neighbourhoods. Risk at this level may be affected by factors such as the existence of a local drug trade, the absence of social networks, and concentrated poverty. All these factors have been shown to be important in several types of violence.
</p><p>Finally, the fourth level looks at the broad societal factors that help to create a climate in which violence is encouraged or inhibited: the responsiveness of the criminal justice system, social and cultural norms regarding gender roles or parent-child relationships, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Income_inequality" title="Income inequality">income inequality</a>, the strength of the social welfare system, the social acceptability of violence, the availability of weapons, the exposure to violence in mass media, and political instability.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child-rearing">Child-rearing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Child-rearing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While studies showing associations between physical punishment of children and later aggression cannot prove that physical punishment causes an increase in aggression, a number of <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_study" title="Longitudinal study">longitudinal studies</a> suggest that the experience of physical punishment has a direct causal effect on later aggressive behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> Cross-cultural studies have shown that greater prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment" title="Corporal punishment">corporal punishment</a> of children tends to predict higher levels of violence in societies. For instance, a 2005 analysis of 186 <a href="/wiki/Pre-industrial_society" title="Pre-industrial society">pre-industrial societies</a> found that corporal punishment was more prevalent in societies which also had higher rates of homicide, assault, and war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> In the United States, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_corporal_punishment" title="Domestic corporal punishment">domestic corporal punishment</a> has been linked to later violent acts against family members and spouses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> The American family violence researcher <a href="/wiki/Murray_A._Straus" title="Murray A. Straus">Murray A. Straus</a> believes that disciplinary <a href="/wiki/Spanking" title="Spanking">spanking</a> forms "the most prevalent and important form of violence in American families", whose effects contribute to several major societal problems, including later domestic violence and crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The causes of violent behavior in people are often a topic of research in <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>. <a href="/wiki/Neuroscientist" title="Neuroscientist">Neurobiologist</a> Jan Vodka emphasizes that, for those purposes, "violent behavior is defined as overt and intentional physically aggressive behavior against another person."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on the idea of human nature, scientists do agree violence is inherent in humans. Among prehistoric humans, there is archaeological evidence for both contentions of violence and peacefulness as primary characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since violence is a matter of perception as well as a measurable phenomenon, psychologists have found variability in whether people perceive certain physical acts as "violent". For example, in a state where execution is a legalized punishment we do not typically perceive the executioner as "violent", though we may talk, in a more metaphorical way, of the state acting violently. Likewise, understandings of violence are linked to a perceived aggressor-victim relationship: hence psychologists have shown that people may not recognise defensive use of force as violent, even in cases where the amount of force used is significantly greater than in the original aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of violence normalization is known as socially sanctioned, or <a href="/wiki/Structural_violence" title="Structural violence">structural violence</a> and is a topic of increasing interest to researchers trying to understand violent behavior. It has been discussed at length by researchers in <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Medical_anthropology" title="Medical anthropology">medical anthropology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bioarchaeology" title="Bioarchaeology">bioarchaeology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">Evolutionary psychology</a> offers several explanations for human violence in various contexts, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexual_jealousy_in_humans" title="Sexual jealousy in humans">sexual jealousy in humans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> child abuse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> Goetz (2010) argues that humans are similar to most <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammal</a> species and use violence in specific situations. He writes that "Buss and Shackelford (1997a) proposed seven adaptive problems our ancestors recurrently faced that might have been solved by aggression: co-opting the resources of others, defending against attack, inflicting costs on same-sex rivals, negotiating status and hierarchies, deterring rivals from future aggression, deterring mate from infidelity, and reducing resources expended on genetically unrelated children."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EP_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-EP-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Goetz writes that most <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicides</a> seem to start from relatively trivial disputes between unrelated men who then escalate to violence and death. He argues that such conflicts occur when there is a status dispute between men of relatively similar status. If there is a great initial status difference, then the lower status individual usually offers no challenge and if challenged the higher status individual usually ignores the lower status individual. At the same an environment of great inequalities between people may cause those at the bottom to use more violence in attempts to gain status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EP_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-EP-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_violence_research" title="Media violence research">Media violence research</a></div>
<p>Research into the media and violence examines whether links between consuming media violence and subsequent aggressive and violent behaviour exists. Although some scholars had claimed media violence may increase aggression,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> this view is coming increasingly in doubt both in the scholarly community<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> and was rejected by the US Supreme Court in the <a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Entertainment_Merchants_Association" title="Brown v. Entertainment Merchants Association">Brown v EMA</a> case, as well as in a review of video game violence by the Australian Government (2010) which concluded evidence for harmful effects were inconclusive at best and the rhetoric of some scholars was not matched by good data.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The threat and enforcement of physical punishment has been a tried and tested method of preventing some violence since civilisation began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> It is used in various degrees in most countries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_awareness_campaigns">Public awareness campaigns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Public awareness campaigns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cities and counties throughout the United States organize "Violence Prevention Months" where the mayor, by proclamation, or the county, by a resolution, encourage the private, community and public sectors to engage in activities that raise awareness that violence is not acceptable through art, music, lectures and events. For example, Violence Prevention Month coordinator, Karen Earle Lile in Contra Costa County, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> created a Wall of Life, where children drew pictures that were put up in the walls of banks and public spaces, displaying a child's view of violence they had witnessed and how it affected them, in an effort to draw attention to how violence affects the community, not just the people involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpersonal_violence">Interpersonal violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Interpersonal violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A review of scientific literature by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> on the effectiveness of strategies to prevent interpersonal violence identified the seven strategies below as being supported by either strong or emerging evidence for effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> These strategies target risk factors at all four levels of the ecological model.
</p>
<h4><span id="Child.E2.80.93caregiver_relationships"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Child–caregiver_relationships">Child–caregiver relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Child–caregiver relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Among the most effective such programmes to prevent child maltreatment and reduce childhood aggression are the Nurse Family Partnership home-visiting programme<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_P_(Parenting_Program)" title="Triple P (Parenting Program)">Triple P (Parenting Program)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> There is also emerging evidence that these programmes reduce convictions and violent acts in adolescence and early adulthood, and probably help decrease intimate partner violence and self-directed violence in later life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_skills_in_youth">Life skills in youth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Life skills in youth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Evidence shows that the <a href="/wiki/Life_skills" title="Life skills">life skills</a> acquired in social development programmes can reduce involvement in violence, improve social skills, boost educational achievement and improve job prospects. Life skills refer to social, emotional, and behavioural competencies which help children and adolescents effectively deal with the challenges of everyday life.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_equality">Gender equality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Gender equality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Evaluation studies are beginning to support community interventions that aim to prevent <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a> by promoting <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a>. For instance, evidence suggests that programmes that combine microfinance with gender equity training can reduce intimate partner violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> School-based programmes such as Safe Dates programme in the United States of America<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> and the Youth Relationship Project in Canada<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup> have been found to be effective for reducing dating violence.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_norms">Cultural norms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Cultural norms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Rules or expectations of behaviour – norms – within a cultural or social group can encourage violence. Interventions that challenge cultural and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_norms" title="Social norms">social norms</a> supportive of violence can prevent acts of violence and have been widely used, but the evidence base for their effectiveness is currently weak. The effectiveness of interventions addressing <a href="/wiki/Dating_violence" title="Dating violence">dating violence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abuse" title="Sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a> among teenagers and young adults by challenging social and cultural norms related to gender is supported by some evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_programmes">Support programmes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Support programmes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Interventions to identify victims of interpersonal violence and provide effective care and support are critical for protecting health and breaking cycles of violence from one generation to the next. Examples for which evidence of effectiveness is emerging includes: screening tools to identify victims of intimate partner violence and refer them to appropriate services;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> psychosocial interventions—such as trauma-focused cognitive behavioural therapy—to reduce mental health problems associated with violence, including post-traumatic stress disorder;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> and protection orders, which prohibit a perpetrator from contacting the victim,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup> to reduce repeat victimization among victims of intimate partner violence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collective_violence">Collective violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Collective violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Not surprisingly, scientific evidence about the effectiveness of interventions to prevent collective violence is lacking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> However, policies that facilitate <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">reductions in poverty</a>, that make <a href="/wiki/Decision-making" title="Decision-making">decision-making</a> more accountable, that reduce inequalities between groups, as well as policies that reduce access to biological, chemical, nuclear and other weapons have been recommended. When planning responses to violent conflicts, recommended approaches include assessing at an early stage who is most vulnerable and what their needs are, co-ordination of activities between various players and working towards global, national and local capabilities so as to deliver effective health services during the various stages of an emergency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_justice">Criminal justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Criminal justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:No_Violence_Sign.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/No_Violence_Sign.jpg/220px-No_Violence_Sign.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/No_Violence_Sign.jpg/330px-No_Violence_Sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/No_Violence_Sign.jpg/440px-No_Violence_Sign.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:No_Violence_Sign.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign that calls to stop violence</div></div></div>
<p>One of the main functions of <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> is to regulate violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup> Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> stated that the state claims the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monopoly_of_the_legitimate_use_of_force" title="Monopoly of the legitimate use of force">monopoly of the legitimate use of force</a> practised within the confines of a specific territory. <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">Law</a> enforcement is the main means of regulating nonmilitary violence in society. Governments regulate the use of violence through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_system" title="Legal system">legal systems</a> governing individuals and political authorities, including the <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a>. Civil societies authorize some amount of violence, exercised through the <a href="/wiki/Police_power_(United_States_constitutional_law)" title="Police power (United States constitutional law)">police power</a>, to maintain the status quo and enforce laws.
</p><p>However, German political theorist <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a> noted: "Violence can be justifiable, but it never will be legitimate ... Its justification loses in plausibility the farther its intended end recedes into the future. No one questions the use of violence in self-defence, because the danger is not only clear but also present, and the end justifying the means is immediate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup> Arendt made a clear distinction between violence and power. Most political theorists regarded violence as an extreme manifestation of power whereas Arendt regarded the two concepts as opposites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup>
In the 20th century in acts of <a href="/wiki/Democide" title="Democide">democide</a> governments may have killed more than 260 million of their own people through <a href="/wiki/Police_brutality" title="Police brutality">police brutality</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Execution" title="Execution">execution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massacre" title="Massacre">massacre</a>, slave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_camps" title="Labour camps">labour camps</a>, and sometimes through intentional <a href="/wiki/List_of_famines#20th_century" title="List of famines">famine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Violent acts that are not carried out by the military or police and that are not in <a href="/wiki/Self-defense" title="Self-defense">self-defense</a> are usually classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimes" title="Crimes">crimes</a>, although not all crimes are <a href="/wiki/Violent_crime" title="Violent crime">violent crimes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Property_damage" title="Property damage">Damage to property</a> is classified as violent crime in some jurisdictions but not in all.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.
