(August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI) classifies violence resulting in <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a> into <a href="/wiki/Murder" title="Murder">criminal homicide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justifiable_homicide" title="Justifiable homicide">justifiable homicide</a> (e.g. self-defense).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The criminal justice approach sees its main task as enforcing laws that proscribe violence and ensuring that "justice is done". The notions of individual blame, responsibility, guilt, and culpability are central to criminal justice's approach to violence and one of the criminal justice system's main tasks is to "do justice", i.e. to ensure that offenders are properly identified, that the degree of their guilt is as accurately ascertained as possible, and that they are punished appropriately. To prevent and respond to violence, the criminal justice approach relies primarily on deterrence, incarceration and the punishment and rehabilitation of perpetrators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The criminal justice approach, beyond justice and punishment, has traditionally emphasized indicated interventions, aimed at those who have already been involved in violence, either as victims or as perpetrators. One of the main reasons offenders are arrested, prosecuted, and convicted is to prevent further crimes—through deterrence (threatening potential offenders with criminal sanctions if they commit crimes), incapacitation (physically preventing offenders from committing further crimes by locking them up) and through rehabilitation (using time spent under state supervision to develop skills or change one's psychological make-up to reduce the likelihood of future offences).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>In recent decades in many countries in the world, the criminal justice system has taken an increasing interest in preventing violence before it occurs. For instance, much of community and <a href="/wiki/Problem-oriented_policing" title="Problem-oriented policing">problem-oriented policing</a> aims to reduce crime and violence by altering the conditions that foster it—and not to increase the number of arrests. Indeed, some police leaders have gone so far as to say the police should primarily be a crime prevention agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup> Juvenile justice systems—an important component of criminal justice systems—are largely based on the belief in rehabilitation and prevention. In the US, the criminal justice system has, for instance, funded school- and community-based initiatives to reduce children's access to guns and teach conflict resolution. Despite this, force is used routinely against juveniles by police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup> In 1974, the US Department of Justice assumed primary responsibility for delinquency prevention programmes and created the Office of Juvenile Justice and Delinquency Prevention, which has supported the "Blueprints for violence prevention" programme at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Colorado_Boulder" title="University of Colorado Boulder">University of Colorado Boulder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health">Public health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Public health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The public health approach is a science-driven, population-based, interdisciplinary, intersectoral approach based on the ecological model which emphasizes primary prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup> Rather than focusing on individuals, the public health approach aims to provide the maximum benefit for the largest number of people, and to extend better care and safety to entire populations. The public health approach is interdisciplinary, drawing upon knowledge from many disciplines including medicine, epidemiology, sociology, psychology, criminology, education and economics. Because all forms of violence are multi-faceted problems, the public health approach emphasizes a multi-sectoral response. It has been proved time and again that cooperative efforts from such diverse sectors as health, education, social welfare, and criminal justice are often necessary to solve what are usually assumed to be purely "criminal" or "medical" problems. The public health approach considers that violence, rather than being the result of any single factor, is the outcome of multiple risk factors and causes, interacting at four levels of a nested hierarchy (individual, close relationship/family, community and wider society) of the <a href="/wiki/Social_ecological_model" title="Social ecological model">Social ecological model</a>.
</p><p>From a public health perspective, prevention strategies can be classified into three types:
</p>
<ul><li>Primary prevention – approaches that aim to prevent violence before it occurs.</li>
<li>Secondary prevention – approaches that focus on the more immediate responses to violence, such as pre-hospital care, emergency services or treatment for sexually transmitted infections following a rape.</li>
<li>Tertiary prevention – approaches that focus on long-term care in the wake of violence, such as rehabilitation and reintegration, and attempt to lessen trauma or reduce long-term disability associated with violence.</li></ul>
<p>A public health approach emphasizes the primary prevention of violence, i.e. stopping them from occurring in the first place. Until recently, this approach has been relatively neglected in the field, with the majority of resources directed towards secondary or tertiary prevention. Perhaps the most critical element of a public health approach to prevention is the ability to identify underlying causes rather than focusing upon more visible "symptoms". This allows for the development and testing of effective approaches to address the underlying causes and so improve health.
</p><p>The public health approach is an evidence-based and systematic process involving the following four steps:
</p>
<ol><li>Defining the problem conceptually and numerically, using statistics that accurately describe the nature and scale of violence, the characteristics of those most affected, the geographical distribution of incidents, and the consequences of exposure to such violence.</li>
<li>Investigating why the problem occurs by determining its causes and correlates, the factors that increase or decrease the risk of its occurrence (risk and protective factors) and the factors that might be modifiable through intervention.</li>
<li>Exploring ways to prevent the problem by using the above information and designing, monitoring and rigorously assessing the effectiveness of programmes through outcome evaluations.</li>
<li>Disseminating information on the effectiveness of programmes and increasing the scale of proven effective programmes. Approaches to prevent violence, whether targeted at individuals or entire communities, must be properly evaluated for their effectiveness and the results shared. This step also includes adapting programmes to local contexts and subjecting them to rigorous re-evaluation to ensure their effectiveness in the new setting.</li></ol>
<p>In many countries, violence prevention is still a new or emerging field in public health. The public health community has started only recently to realize the contributions it can make to reducing violence and mitigating its consequences. In 1949, Gordon called for injury prevention efforts to be based on the understanding of causes, in a similar way to prevention efforts for communicable and other diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup> In 1962, Gomez, referring to the WHO definition of health, stated that it is obvious that violence does not contribute to "extending life" or to a "complete state of well-being". He defined violence as an issue that public health experts needed to address and stated that it should not be the primary domain of lawyers, military personnel, or politicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, it is only in the last 30 years that public health has begun to address violence, and only in the last fifteen has it done so at the global level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup> This is a much shorter period of time than public health has been tackling other health problems of comparable magnitude and with similarly severe lifelong consequences.
</p><p>The global public health response to interpersonal violence began in earnest in the mid-1990s. In 1996, the World Health Assembly adopted Resolution WHA49.25<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> which declared violence "a leading worldwide public health problem" and requested that the World Health Organization (WHO) initiate public health activities to (1) document and characterize the burden of violence, (2) assess the effectiveness of programmes, with particular attention to women and children and community-based initiatives, and (3) promote activities to tackle the problem at the international and national levels. The World Health Organization's initial response to this resolution was to create the Department of Violence and Injury Prevention and Disability and to publish the World report on violence and health (2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The case for the public health sector addressing interpersonal violence rests on four main arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup> First, the significant amount of time health care professionals dedicate to caring for victims and perpetrators of violence has made them familiar with the problem and has led many, particularly in emergency departments, to mobilize to address it. The information, resources, and infrastructures the health care sector has at its disposal are an important asset for research and prevention work. Second, the magnitude of the problem and its potentially severe lifelong consequences and high costs to individuals and wider society call for population-level interventions typical of the public health approach. Third, the criminal justice approach, the other main approach to addressing violence (link to entry above), has traditionally been more geared towards violence that occurs between male youths and adults in the street and other public places—which makes up the bulk of homicides in most countries—than towards violence occurring in private settings such as child maltreatment, intimate partner violence and elder abuse—which makes up the largest share of non-fatal violence. Fourth, evidence is beginning to accumulate that a science-based public health approach is effective at preventing interpersonal violence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="1277" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg/220px-Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg/330px-Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg/440px-Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abdulredha_Buhmaid_on_floor.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>'s pro-democracy protesters killed by military, February 2011</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> approach is based on the obligations of states to respect, protect and fulfill human rights and therefore to prevent, eradicate and punish violence. It recognizes violence as a violation of many human rights: the rights to life, liberty, <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a> and security of the person; the rights to equality and non-discrimination; the rights to be free from torture and cruel, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inhuman_or_degrading_treatment" title="Inhuman or degrading treatment">inhuman and degrading treatment</a> or punishment; the right to <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_health" title="Right to health">right to the highest attainable standard of health</a>. These human rights are enshrined in <a href="/wiki/International_human_rights_law" title="International human rights law">international and regional treaties</a> and national constitutions and laws, which stipulate the obligations of states, and include mechanisms to hold states accountable. The <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Discrimination_Against_Women" title="Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women">Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women</a>, for example, requires that countries party to the Convention take all appropriate steps to end violence against women. The <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Rights_of_the_Child" title="Convention on the Rights of the Child">Convention on the Rights of the Child</a> in its Article 19 states that States Parties shall take all appropriate legislative, administrative, social and educational measures to protect the child from all forms of physical or mental violence, injury or abuse, neglect or negligent treatment, maltreatment or exploitation, including <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a>, while in the care of parent(s), legal guardian(s) or any other person who has the care of the child.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_context">Geographical context</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Geographical context">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Violence, as defined in the dictionary of human geography, "appears whenever power is in jeopardy" and "in and of itself stands emptied of strength and purpose: it is part of a larger matrix of socio-political power struggles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyndman,_J._2009_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyndman,_J._2009-124">[121]</a></sup> Violence can be broadly divided into three broad categories—direct violence, structural violence and cultural violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyndman,_J._2009_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hyndman,_J._2009-124">[121]</a></sup> Thus defined and delineated, it is of note, as Hyndman says, that "geography came late to theorizing violence"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyndman,_J._2009_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hyndman,_J._2009-124">[121]</a></sup> in comparison to other social sciences. Social and human geography, rooted in the <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxist" title="Marxist">Marxist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory" title="Feminist theory">feminist</a> subfields that emerged following the early positivist approaches and subsequent behavioral turn, have long been concerned with social and <a href="/wiki/Spatial_justice" title="Spatial justice">spatial justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup>
Along with critical geographers and political geographers, it is these groupings of geographers that most often interact with violence. Keeping this idea of social/spatial justice via geography in mind, it is worthwhile to look at geographical approaches to violence in the context of politics.
</p><p>Derek Gregory and Alan Pred assembled the influential edited collection <i>Violent Geographies: Fear, Terror, and Political Violence</i>, which demonstrates how place, space, and landscape are foremost factors in the real and imagined practices of organized violence both historically and in the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup> Evidently, political violence often gives a part for the state to play. When "modern states not only claim a monopoly of the legitimate means of violence; they also routinely use the threat of violence to enforce the rule of law",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyndman,_J._2009_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hyndman,_J._2009-124">[121]</a></sup> the law not only becomes a form of violence but is violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyndman,_J._2009_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hyndman,_J._2009-124">[121]</a></sup> Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Agamben" title="Giorgio Agamben">Giorgio Agamben</a>'s concepts of <a href="/wiki/State_of_exception" title="State of exception">state of exception</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sacer" title="Homo sacer">homo sacer</a></i> are useful to consider within a geography of violence. The state, in the grip of a perceived, potential crisis (whether legitimate or not) takes preventative legal measures, such as a suspension of rights (it is in this climate, as Agamben demonstrates, that the formation of the Social Democratic and Nazi government's lager or concentration camp can occur). However, when this "in limbo" reality is designed to be in place "until further notice…the state of exception thus ceases to be referred to as an external and provisional state of factual danger and comes to be confused with juridical rule itself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agamben,_G._1998_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agamben,_G._1998-127">[124]</a></sup> For Agamben, the physical space of the camp "is a piece of land placed outside the normal juridical order, but it is nevertheless not simply an external space".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agamben,_G._1998_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Agamben,_G._1998-127">[124]</a></sup> At the scale of the body, in the state of exception, a person is so removed from their rights by "juridical procedures and deployments of power"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agamben,_G._1998_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Agamben,_G._1998-127">[124]</a></sup> that "no act committed against them could appear any longer as a crime";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agamben,_G._1998_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-Agamben,_G._1998-127">[124]</a></sup> in other words, people become only <i>homo sacer</i>. <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_detention_camp" title="Guantanamo Bay detention camp">Guantanamo Bay</a> could also be said to represent the physicality of the state of exception in space, and can just as easily draw man as homo sacer.
</p><p>In the 1970s, genocides in Cambodia under the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge" title="Khmer Rouge">Khmer Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pol_Pot" title="Pol Pot">Pol Pot</a> resulted in the deaths of over two million Cambodians (which was 25% of the Cambodian population), forming one of the many contemporary examples of state-sponsored violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ringer,_G._2002_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ringer,_G._2002-128">[125]</a></sup> About fourteen thousand of these murders occurred at <a href="/wiki/Choeung_Ek" title="Choeung Ek">Choeung Ek</a>, which is the best-known of the extermination camps referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Killing_Fields" title="Killing Fields">Killing Fields</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ringer,_G._2002_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ringer,_G._2002-128">[125]</a></sup> The killings were arbitrary; for example, a person could be killed for wearing glasses, since that was seen as associating them with intellectuals and therefore as making them part of the enemy. People were murdered with impunity because it was no crime; Cambodians were made <i>homo sacer</i> in a condition of bare life. The Killing Fields—manifestations of Agamben's concept of camps beyond the normal rule of law—featured the state of exception. As part of Pol Pot's "ideological intent…to create a purely agrarian society or cooperative",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ringer,_G._2002_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ringer,_G._2002-128">[125]</a></sup> he "dismantled the country's existing economic infrastructure and depopulated every urban area".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ringer,_G._2002_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ringer,_G._2002-128">[125]</a></sup> Forced movement, such as this forced movement applied by Pol Pot, is a clear display of structural violence. When "symbols of Cambodian society were equally disrupted, social institutions of every kind…were purged or torn down",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ringer,_G._2002_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ringer,_G._2002-128">[125]</a></sup> cultural violence (defined as when "any aspect of culture such as language, religion, ideology, art, or cosmology is used to legitimize direct or structural violence"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyndman,_J._2009_124-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hyndman,_J._2009-124">[121]</a></sup>) is added to the structural violence of forced movement and to the direct violence, such as murder, at the Killing Fields. Vietnam eventually intervened and the genocide officially ended. However, ten million landmines left by opposing guerillas in the 1970s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ringer,_G._2002_128-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ringer,_G._2002-128">[125]</a></sup> continue to create a violent landscape in Cambodia.
</p><p>Human geography, though coming late to the theorizing table, has tackled violence through many lenses, including anarchist geography, feminist geography, Marxist geography, political geography, and critical geography. However, <a href="/wiki/Adriana_Cavarero" title="Adriana Cavarero">Adriana Cavarero</a> notes that, "as violence spreads and assumes unheard-of forms, it becomes difficult to name in contemporary language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cavarero,_A._2009_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cavarero,_A._2009-129">[126]</a></sup> Cavarero proposes that, in facing such a truth, it is prudent to reconsider violence as "horrorism"; that is, "as though ideally all the…victims, instead of their killers, ought to determine the name".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cavarero,_A._2009_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cavarero,_A._2009-129">[126]</a></sup> With geography often adding the forgotten spatial aspect to theories of social science, rather than creating them solely within the discipline, it seems that the self-reflexive contemporary geography of today may have an extremely important place in this current (re)imaging of violence, exemplified by Cavarero.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff5800; color:black;"> </span> 1600–1800</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff4200; color:black;"> </span> 1800–2000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff2c00; color:black;"> </span> 2000–3000</div> <link <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/290px-Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/435px-Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/580px-Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interpersonal_violence_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deaths due to interpersonal violence per million persons in 2012 <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1054258005" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="refbegin refbegin-columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="column-count: 3;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff20; color:black;"> </span> 0–8</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffe820; color:black;"> </span> 9–16</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffd820; color:black;"> </span> 17–24</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc020; color:black;"> </span> 25–32</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffa020; color:black;"> </span> 33–54</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a20; color:black;"> </span> 55–75</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f08015; color:black;"> </span> 76–96</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e06815; color:black;"> </span> 97–126</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d85010; color:black;"> </span> 127–226</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 227–878</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>As of 2010, all forms of violence resulted in about 1.34 million deaths up from about 1 million in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loz2012_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loz2012-131">[128]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">Suicide</a> accounts for about 883,000, interpersonal violence for 456,000 and collective violence for 18,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loz2012_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Loz2012-131">[128]</a></sup> Deaths due to collective violence have decreased from 64,000 in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loz2012_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-Loz2012-131">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>By way of comparison, the 1.5 millions deaths a year due to violence is greater than the number of deaths due to tuberculosis (1.34 million), road traffic injuries (1.21 million), and malaria (830'000), but slightly less than the number of people who die from HIV/AIDS (1.77 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>For every death due to violence, there are numerous nonfatal injuries. In 2008, over 16 million cases of non-fatal violence-related injuries were severe enough to require medical attention. Beyond deaths and injuries, forms of violence such as child maltreatment, intimate partner violence, and elder maltreatment have been found to be highly prevalent.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-directed_violence">Self-directed violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Self-directed violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the last 45 years, suicide rates have increased by 60% worldwide.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Suicide is among the three leading causes of death among those aged 15–44 years in some countries, and the second leading cause of death in the 10–24 years age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> These figures do not include suicide attempts which are up to 20 times more frequent than completed suicide.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Suicide was the 16th leading cause of death worldwide in 2004 and is projected to increase to the 12th in 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup> Although suicide rates have traditionally been highest among the male elderly, rates among young people have been increasing to such an extent that they are now the group at highest risk in a third of countries, in both developed and developing countries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpersonal_violence_2">Interpersonal violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Interpersonal violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rates and patterns of violent death vary by country and region. In recent years, homicide rates have been highest in developing countries in Sub-Saharan Africa and Latin America and the Caribbean and lowest in East Asia, the western Pacific, and some countries in northern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup> Studies show a strong, inverse relationship between homicide rates and both economic development and economic equality. Poorer countries, especially those with large gaps between the rich and the poor, tend to have higher rates of homicide than wealthier countries. Homicide rates differ markedly by age and sex. Gender differences are least marked for children. For the 15 to 29 age group, male rates were nearly six times those for female rates; for the remaining age groups, male rates were from two to four times those for females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies in a number of countries show that, for every homicide among young people age 10 to 24, 20 to 40 other young people receive hospital treatment for a violent injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Forms of violence such as child maltreatment and intimate partner violence are highly prevalent. Approximately 20% of women and 5–10% of men report being sexually abused as children, while 25–50% of all children report being physically abused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup> A WHO multi-country study found that between 15 and 71% of women reported experiencing physical and/or sexual violence by an intimate partner at some point in their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collective_violence_2">Collective violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Collective violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wars grab headlines, but the individual risk of dying violently in an armed conflict is today relatively low—much lower than the risk of violent death in many countries that are not suffering from an armed conflict. For example, between 1976 and 2008, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African Americans</a> were victims of 329,825 homicides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup> Although there is a widespread perception that war is the most dangerous form of armed violence in the world, the average person living in a conflict-affected country had a risk of dying violently in the conflict of about 2.0 per 100,000 population between 2004 and 2007. This can be compared to the average world homicide rate of 7.6 per 100,000 people. This illustration highlights the value of accounting for all forms of armed violence rather than an exclusive focus on conflict related violence. Certainly, there are huge variations in the risk of dying from armed conflict at the national and subnational level, and the risk of dying violently in a conflict in specific countries remains extremely high. In Iraq, for example, the direct conflict death rate for 2004–07 was 65 per 100,000 people per year and, in Somalia, 24 per 100,000 people. This rate even reached peaks of 91 per 100,000 in Iraq in 2006 and 74 per 100,000 in Somalia in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"History of violence" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/History_of_violence_(disambiguation)" title="History of violence (disambiguation)">History of violence (disambiguation)</a>.</div><p>
Scientific evidence for warfare has come from settled, sedentary communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theverge.com_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-theverge.com-142">[139]</a></sup> Some scholars argue humans may have a predisposition for violence (chimpanzees, also great apes, have been known to kill members of competing groups for resources like food),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theverge.com_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-theverge.com-142">[139]</a></sup> placing the origins of organized violence prior to modern settled societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theverge.com_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-theverge.com-142">[139]</a></sup> However, actual evidence suggests that organized, large-scale, militaristic, or regular human-on-human violence was absent for the vast majority of the human timeline,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly-145">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup> and is first documented to have started only relatively recently in the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>, an epoch that began about 11,700 years ago, probably with the advent of higher population densities due to <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly-145">[142]</a></sup> Social anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Douglas_P._Fry" title="Douglas P. Fry">Douglas P. Fry</a> writes that scholars are divided on the origins of this greater degree of violence—in other words, war-like behavior: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There are basically two schools of thought on this issue. One holds that warfare... goes back at least to the time of the first thoroughly modern humans and even before then to the primate ancestors of the hominid lineage. The second positions on the origins of warfare sees war as much less common in the cultural and biological evolution of humans. Here, warfare is a latecomer on the cultural horizon, only arising in very specific material circumstances and being quite rare in human history until the development of agriculture in the past 10,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a> in his books <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guns,_Germs_and_Steel" title="Guns, Germs and Steel">Guns, Germs and Steel</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Third_Chimpanzee" title="The Third Chimpanzee">The Third Chimpanzee</a></i> posits that the rise of large-scale warfare is the result of advances in technology and city-states. For instance, the rise of agriculture provided a significant increase in the number of individuals that a region could sustain over hunter-gatherer societies, allowing for development of specialized classes such as soldiers, or weapons manufacturers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="704" data-file-width="1173" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg/220px-War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg/330px-War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg/440px-War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:War_deaths_caused_by_warfare.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The percentages of men killed in war in eight tribal societies. (Lawrence H. Keeley, Archeologist, <i><a href="/wiki/War_Before_Civilization" title="War Before Civilization">War Before Civilization</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>In academia, the idea of the peaceful pre-history and non-violent tribal societies gained popularity with the <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">post-colonial perspective</a>. The trend, starting in <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> and spreading to <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a> reached its height in the late half of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keely1_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keely1-148">[145]</a></sup> However, some newer research in archaeology and <a href="/wiki/Bioarchaeology" title="Bioarchaeology">bioarchaeology</a> may provide evidence that violence within and among groups is not a recent phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup> According to the book "The Bioarchaeology of Violence" violence is a behavior that is found throughout human history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lawrence_H._Keeley" title="Lawrence H. Keeley">Lawrence H. Keeley</a> at the University of Illinois writes in <i><a href="/wiki/War_Before_Civilization" title="War Before Civilization">War Before Civilization</a></i> that 87% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribal_societies" title="Tribal societies">tribal societies</a> were at war more than once per year, and that 65% of them were fighting continuously. He writes that the attrition rate of numerous close-quarter clashes, which characterize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic_warfare" title="Endemic warfare">endemic warfare</a>, produces casualty rates of up to 60%, compared to 1% of the combatants as is typical in modern warfare. "Primitive Warfare" of these small groups or tribes was driven by the basic need for sustenance and violent competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fry explores Keeley's argument in depth and counters that such sources erroneously focus on the ethnography of hunters and gatherers in the present, whose culture and values have been infiltrated externally by modern civilization, rather than the actual archaeological record spanning some two million years of human existence. Fry determines that all present ethnographically studied tribal societies, "by the very fact of having been described and published by anthropologists, have been irrevocably impacted by history and modern colonial nation states" and that "many have been affected by state societies for at least 5000 years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relatively peaceful period since <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> is known as the <a href="/wiki/Long_Peace" title="Long Peace">Long Peace</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Better_Angels_of_Our_Nature"><i>The Better Angels of Our Nature</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: The Better Angels of Our Nature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a>'s 2011 book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Better_Angels_of_Our_Nature" title="The Better Angels of Our Nature">The Better Angels of Our Nature</a></i>, argued that modern society is less violent than in periods of the past, whether on the short scale of decades or long scale of centuries or millennia.
</p><p>Steven Pinker argues that by every possible measure, every type of violence has drastically decreased since ancient and medieval times. A few centuries ago, for example, <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a> was a standard practice in all kinds of warfare and was so common that historians did not even bother to mention it. <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">Cannibalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> have been greatly reduced in the last thousand years, and <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">capital punishment</a> is now banned in many countries. According to Pinker, rape, murder, warfare and animal cruelty have all seen drastic declines in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinker_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinker-153">[150]</a></sup> Pinker's analyses have also been criticized, concerning the statistical question of how to measure violence and whether it is in fact declining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laws_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laws-155">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pinker's observation of the decline in interpersonal violence echoes the work of <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Elias" title="Norbert Elias">Norbert Elias</a>, who attributes the decline to a "civilizing process", in which the state's monopolization of violence, the maintenance of socioeconomic interdependencies or "figurations", and the maintenance of behavioural codes in culture all contribute to the development of individual sensibilities, which increase the repugnance of individuals towards violent acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> According to a 2010 study, non-lethal violence, such as assaults or bullying appear to be declining as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some scholars disagree with the argument that all violence is decreasing arguing that not all types of violent behaviour are lower now than in the past. They suggest that research typically focuses on lethal violence, often looks at <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a> rates of death due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a>, but ignore the less obvious forms of violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot;Stop_Violencequot;_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot%3BStop_Violencequot%3B_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG/220px-PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot%3BStop_Violencequot%3B_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot%3BStop_Violencequot%3B_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG/330px-PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot%3BStop_Violencequot%3B_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot%3BStop_Violencequot%3B_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG/440px-PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot%3BStop_Violencequot%3B_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PikiWiki_Israel_20285_quot;Stop_Violencequot;_sculpture_in_Petah_Tikva.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sculpture in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petah_Tikva,_Israel" title="Petah Tikva, Israel">Petah Tikva, Israel</a> of a padlock on the warped barrel of a <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_pistol" title="Semi-automatic pistol">semi-automatic pistol</a>, with the inscription "stop violence!" in (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he">!די לאלימות</span>)</div></div></div>
<p>Beyond deaths and injuries, highly prevalent forms of violence (such as child maltreatment and intimate partner violence) have serious lifelong non-injury health consequences. Victims may engage in high-risk behaviours such as alcohol and substance misuse and smoking, which in turn can contribute to cardiovascular disorders, cancers, depression, diabetes and HIV/AIDS, resulting in premature death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup> The balances of prevention, mitigation, mediation and exacerbation are complex, and vary with the underpinnings of violence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_effects">Economic effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Economic effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In countries with high levels of violence, economic growth can be slowed down, personal and collective security eroded, and social development impeded. Families edging out of poverty and investing in schooling their sons and daughters can be ruined through the violent death or severe disability of the main breadwinner. Communities can be caught in poverty traps where pervasive violence and deprivation form a vicious circle that stifles economic growth. For societies, meeting the direct costs of health, criminal justice, and social welfare responses to violence diverts many billions of dollars from more constructive societal spending. The much larger indirect costs of violence due to lost productivity and lost investment in education work together to slow economic development, increase socioeconomic inequality, and erode human and social capital.
</p><p>Additionally, communities with high level of violence do not provide the level of stability and predictability vital for a prospering business economy. Individuals will be less likely to invest money and effort towards growth in such unstable and violent conditions. One of the possible proves might be the study of <a href="/wiki/Baten" title="Baten">Baten</a> and Gust that used "<a href="/wiki/Regicide" title="Regicide">regicide</a>" as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Measurement_unit" title="Measurement unit">measurement unit</a> to approximate the influence of interpersonal violence and depict the influence of high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpersonal_violence" title="Interpersonal violence">interpersonal violence</a> on <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a> and level of <a href="/wiki/Investment" title="Investment">investments</a>. The results of the research prove the <a href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Human_capital" title="Human capital">human capital</a> and the interpersonal violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Economics_and_Peace" title="Institute for Economics and Peace">Institute for Economics and Peace</a>, released the <a class="external text" href="http://visionofhumanity.org/app/uploads/2017/02/The-Economic-Value-of-Peace-2016-WEB.pdf" rel="nofollow">Economic Value of Peace</a> report, which estimates the economic impact of violence and conflict on the global economy, the total economic impact of violence on the world economy in 2015 was estimated to be $13.6 trillion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> terms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_politics">Religion and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Religion and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence" title="Religious violence">Religious violence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg/220px-Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg/330px-Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg/440px-Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taliban_beating_woman_in_public_RAWA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> beating woman in public</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9,_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg/220px-La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg/330px-La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg/440px-La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9,_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/St._Bartholomew%27s_Day_massacre" title="St. Bartholomew's Day massacre">St. Bartholomew's Day massacre</a> of French Protestants, 1572</div></div></div>
<p>Religious and political ideologies have been the cause of interpersonal violence throughout history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup> Ideologues often falsely accuse others of violence, such as the ancient <a href="/wiki/Blood_libel" title="Blood libel">blood libel</a> against Jews, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> accusations of casting <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft" title="Witchcraft">witchcraft</a> spells against women, and modern accusations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satanic_ritual_abuse" title="Satanic ritual abuse">satanic ritual abuse</a> against day care center owners and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both supporters and opponents of the 21st-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terrorism</a> regard it largely as an ideological and religious war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOXBUMPER_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOXBUMPER-165">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vittorio Bufacchi describes two different modern concepts of violence, one the "minimalist conception" of violence as an intentional act of excessive or destructive force, the other the "comprehensive conception" which includes violations of rights, including a long list of human needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-capitalist" title="Anti-capitalist">Anti-capitalists</a> say that <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> is violent, that <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a> and <a href="/wiki/Profit_(economics)" title="Profit (economics)">profit</a> survive only because police violence defends them, and that capitalist economies need war to expand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[164]</a></sup> In this view, capitalism results in a form of <a href="/wiki/Structural_violence" title="Structural violence">structural violence</a> that stems from inequality, environmental damage, and the exploitation of women and people of color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Frantz_Fanon" title="Frantz Fanon">Frantz Fanon</a> critiqued the violence of <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> and wrote about the counter violence of the "colonized victims."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout history, most religions and individuals like <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> have preached that humans are capable of eliminating individual violence and organizing societies through purely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonviolent" title="Nonviolent">nonviolent</a> means. Gandhi himself once wrote: "A society organized and run on the basis of complete non-violence would be the purest <a href="/wiki/Anarchy" title="Anarchy">anarchy</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup> Modern political ideologies which espouse similar views include pacifist varieties of <a href="/wiki/Voluntaryism" title="Voluntaryism">voluntarism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(economic_theory)" title="Mutualism (economic theory)">mutualism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarianism</a>.
</p><p>Terence Fretheim writing about the Old Testament:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For many people, ... only physical violence truly qualifies as violence. But, certainly, violence is more than killing people, unless one includes all those words and actions that kill people slowly. The effect of limitation to a "killing fields" perspective is the widespread neglect of many other forms of violence. We must insist that violence also refers to that which is psychologically destructive, that which demeans, damages, or depersonalizes others. In view of these considerations, violence may be defined as follows: any action, verbal or nonverbal, oral or written, physical or psychical, active or passive, public or private, individual or institutional/societal, human or divine, in whatever degree of intensity, that abuses, violates, injures, or kills. Some of the most pervasive and most dangerous forms of violence are those that are often hidden from view (against women and children, especially); just beneath the surface in many of our homes, churches, and communities is abuse enough to freeze the
blood. Moreover, many forms of systemic violence often slip past our attention because they are so much a part of the infrastructure of life (e.g., racism, sexism, ageism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fretheim_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fretheim-174">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Violence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-knowledge-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-knowledge_59-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">'any interpretation of reality is always a form of violence in the sense that knowledge "can only be a violation of the things to be known" ... Several philosophers following Nietzsche, Heidegger, Foucault, and Derrida have emphasized and explicated this fundamental violence.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foucault_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-foucault-58">[58]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"While the ontological violence of language does, in significant ways, sustain, enable, and encourage physical violence, it is a serious mistake to conflate them. [...] Violence is understood to be ineliminable in the first sense, and this leads to its being treated as a fundamental in the second sense, too.""<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foucault_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-foucault-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 36">: 36 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-epistemic-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-epistemic_62-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Epistemic violence in testimony is a refusal, intentional or unintentional, of an  audience  to  communicatively  reciprocate  a  linguistic  exchange  owing  to pernicious  ignorance"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dotson_2011_pp._236–257_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dotson_2011_pp._236–257-61">[59]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
